**USER**: So, explain to me, what is a git apply, and what is a git patch?

**ASSISTANT**: Alright, here's the simple breakdown:

A **Git patch** is like a recipeâ€”itâ€™s a text file that records the exact changes made to a file or set of files (like added, removed, or changed lines). Itâ€™s often created with `git format-patch`.

Now, **git apply** is like using that recipeâ€”it takes the patch and applies those changes to your working directory, but *doesnâ€™t* make a commit unless you do that separately afterward.

Itâ€™s super useful if someone sends you a patch file, and you want to try it out without messing with history yet. Youâ€™d just run `git apply patchfile.patch`.

Want to see an example?

**USER**: Sure, but there's so many things in this chat now, it's not even funny. Different subjects, blah blah blah. Yes, go ahead. Give me a patch.

**ASSISTANT**: Totally get itâ€”this chat is a wild jungle of threads. Alright, here's a tiny example of a Git patch and how you'd apply it.

Let's say you have a file called `hello.txt` with this line:

```
Hello, world!
```

And we want to change it to:

```
Hello, universe!
```

A patch file (`change-hello.patch`) for that might look like:

```
diff --git a/hello.txt b/hello.txt
index e69de29..b7d7fdd 100644
--- a/hello.txt
+++ b/hello.txt
@@ -1 +1 @@
-Hello, world!
+Hello, universe!
```

To use it:

1. Save that text to a file, like `change-hello.patch`.
2. Then from the root of your Git repo, run:

```
git apply change-hello.patch
```

Boomâ€”your working directory updates, but no commit yet. Want me to show how to generate one from your own changes next?

**USER**: Would I have to use the command line Git, or can I use GitHub Desktop?

**ASSISTANT**: You can absolutely use GitHub Desktopâ€”it just handles patches differently. It doesnâ€™t support `git apply` directly through the GUI, but hereâ€™s a workaround:

You can make your changes in GitHub Desktop *manually*, then **commit** them, and from there:

1. Click **Repository > Show in Explorer** to locate the repo.
2. Use the Git command line (Git Bash or Terminal) **inside that folder** to run `git apply patchname.patch` if needed.

But if you're trying to save or share a patch of your changes (like a diff), then that part has to be done via command line:

```
git diff > my-changes.patch
```

**USER**: What is this chip

**ASSISTANT**: That component is a **4N35 optocoupler** (also called an optoisolator), manufactured by **ON Semiconductor** (now part of onsemi).

---

### ğŸ”§ **What it does:**
The **4N35** optocoupler is used to **electrically isolate** different parts of a circuit while still allowing an electrical signal to pass through using light.

---

### ğŸ§  **How it works:**
- Inside the chip is an **infrared LED** and a **phototransistor**.
- When current passes through the LED, it emits infrared light.
- The phototransistor detects the light and switches on, passing a signal â€” but **no electrical current crosses over** between the input and output sides.

---

### ğŸ› ï¸ **Typical uses:**
- Microcontroller-to-high voltage isolation
- Signal isolation between digital logic levels
- Switching power devices (like MOSFETs or relays)
- Protecting sensitive circuits from voltage spikes

---

### ğŸ“‹ **Pinout (6-pin DIP):**
```
       _______
      |       |
  1 --|       |-- 6  (Collector)
  2 --|  4N35 |-- 5  (Base - optional use)
  3 --|       |-- 4  (Emitter)
      |_______|
```
- **1:** Anode (LED)
- **2:** Cathode (LED)
- **3:** NC or grounded
- **4:** Emitter (phototransistor)
- **5:** Base (can be left unconnected)
- **6:** Collector (output signal)

---

Would you like a test circuit or schematic to use it with an Arduino or something else?

**USER**: How would a hobbies or a DIYer use it

**ASSISTANT**: Great question â€” and this little guy is **very hobbyist-friendly**. ğŸ§°

Hereâ€™s how a DIYer might use the **4N35 optocoupler** in real-world projects:

---

### ğŸ”‹ **1. Microcontroller Isolation**
**Use Case:** Youâ€™re driving a relay or other high-voltage device from an Arduino, Raspberry Pi, or ESP32.

**Why use it:** It protects your microcontroller from surges, voltage spikes, or back EMF.

**How:**
- Connect the **LED side** (pins 1 and 2) to your Arduinoâ€™s digital output through a current-limiting resistor.
- Connect the **phototransistor side** (pins 4 and 5 or 6) to a separate high-voltage switching circuit (like a relay driver).

ğŸ’¡ Itâ€™s like sending a secret handshake through light instead of electricity.

---

### ğŸ’¡ **2. Signal Level Shifting**
**Use Case:** Youâ€™re trying to communicate between a **3.3V device and a 12V logic system**.

**Why use it:** Optical isolation means voltage levels donâ€™t directly touch â€” so your logic levels donâ€™t get fried.

---

### âš ï¸ **3. Safety Barrier**
**Use Case:** Home automation, AC control, switching motors.

**Why use it:** Keeps **mains AC** totally isolated from your low-voltage microcontroller logic.  
Used often with **solid-state relays** or **zero-crossing detection** circuits.

---

### ğŸ“¦ **4. Audio or Sensor Isolation**
**Use Case:** Preventing ground loops in audio gear or analog sensors.

**Why use it:** Avoids weird hums or phantom readings due to ground potential differences.

---

### ğŸ§ª **Basic DIY Circuit Example:**

```text
[ Arduino Digital Pin ]
         |
         R
         |
        |>|  â† LED side (1N35)
         |
        GND

         |   â† Phototransistor side
        |/
 Vcc ---|    â†’ to transistor/relay/mosfet base or digital input
        |\
         |
        GND
```

- Resistor on the LED side: ~220â€“330Î© for 5V logic
- Pull-up resistor on collector side may be needed

---

### ğŸ§° Project Ideas:
- Isolated relay driver for appliances
- Isolated button press (like â€œremote pressingâ€ a button)
- Digital IR remote clone with safety buffer
- Tachometer sensor for a fan or motor (optically isolated pulse reader)

---

Want help breadboarding something with it? Or want me to mock up a real-world circuit schematic for a project idea youâ€™ve got?

**USER**: Give me a list of projects starting from extreme beginner to NASA scientist using a breadboard

**ASSISTANT**: ğŸ¶ Oh yes â€” **Project 7: Opto-Isolated MIDI IN Interface** â€” a classic and **super-useful** application of the 4N35 (or similar optocoupler). If you're into synths, digital music, or DIY audio gear, this is gold.

---

## ğŸ›ï¸ **Why MIDI Needs Opto-Isolation**
MIDI (Musical Instrument Digital Interface) was designed in the early 1980s to **connect keyboards, synthesizers, drum machines, and computers** safely.

â¡ï¸ **MIDI always uses opto-isolators on the receiving end** to:

- Prevent ground loops
- Avoid damage between different manufacturersâ€™ gear
- Ensure electrical noise doesn't pass between devices

âš¡ Itâ€™s required by the official MIDI spec.

---

## ğŸ”Œ **What MIDI Signals Are**
- 31,250 bits per second (yes, weird baud rate)
- Current-loop system: uses 5 mA current via 220Î© resistors
- 5-pin DIN connector (MIDI IN, OUT, THRU)

---

## ğŸ› ï¸ **MIDI IN Schematic Using a 4N35**
Hereâ€™s a typical schematic for a MIDI IN circuit using your 4N35:

```text
        +5V (receiver side)
           |
           R1 (220Î©)
           |
MIDI Pin 4 ----->|----+
                 LED  |
                     |   Inside 4N35
MIDI Pin 5 ----->|----+--- GND

                 ||  photo
                 ||  transistor
                /  \
               |    |
               |    R2 (10k pull-up)
               |    |
               +----+----> to MCU (MIDI RX)
               |
              GND
```

- **Pin 4 and 5 of MIDI DIN** go to the LED side of the 4N35 with a 220Î© resistor.
- The phototransistor collector connects to +5V via a 10k pull-up.
- The emitter goes to ground.
- Output is taken from the collector to your UART RX pin on a microcontroller.

---

## ğŸ§° **Parts List**
- 4N35 (or 6N138 if you need faster switching)
- 5-pin DIN jack (panel-mount or board-mount)
- 220Î© resistor (current limit on LED side)
- 10kÎ© pull-up resistor (on transistor side)
- Optional: 1N4148 protection diode across the LED

---

## ğŸ›ï¸ **What You Can Build With This**
- âœ… A **MIDI IN interface** for Arduino/Teensy/ESP32 (send notes into your synth code)
- âœ… A **MIDI-to-CV converter** for modular synths
- âœ… A **MIDI-controlled light show or automation rig**
- âœ… A **custom MIDI controller** with real keyboard input

---

## ğŸ§  Bonus: Why Not Use a 4N35 for MIDI OUT?
You donâ€™t. The transmitter side (MIDI OUT) just sends a signal â€” **no isolation is required**. You *could* use one for extra protection, but itâ€™s not standard.

---

## âš¡ Gotchas
- 4N35 is slow: good enough for MIDI, but **use a 6N138 or 6N137** for tighter baud rates or long cables
- Long MIDI cables (over 20 feet) may need proper shielding

---

**USER**: Can you build an Arduino from scratch without an Arduino IC

**ASSISTANT**: Yes â€” **you *can* build an Arduino-compatible circuit from scratch without using a pre-branded Arduino IC** like the ATmega328P with Arduino bootloader. But there are some things to understand depending on how â€œfrom scratchâ€ you mean.

---

## ğŸ”§ 3 Main Paths to "Arduino Without Arduino"

### ğŸŸ¢ **1. Use a raw microcontroller (e.g. ATmega328P without bootloader)**
âœ… Yes, totally possible.

Youâ€™ll need to:
- Flash the Arduino bootloader yourself using **another Arduino as ISP**
- Add:
  - 16 MHz crystal (or run internal 8 MHz clock)
  - 2 Ã— 22pF caps
  - Reset pull-up resistor (10k)
  - Decoupling caps (0.1Î¼F)
  - Power supply (5V regulated)

ğŸ§  Bonus: This is whatâ€™s inside most Arduino UNO boards â€” just a bare ATmega328P.

---

### ğŸŸ¡ **2. Use a different microcontroller (e.g. ATTiny85, STM32, RP2040)**
âœ… Also possible.

But:
- Youâ€™ll need to configure **board support** in the Arduino IDE
- Install **USB-to-serial interface** if it doesnâ€™t have one
- Learn how to:
  - Flash firmware (often with a programmer like USBasp or FTDI)
  - Wire your own power/reset/programming headers

ğŸ’¡ Example: People build **tiny Arduino projects** with ATTiny85 (Digispark) and STM32 Blue Pill boards.

---

### ğŸ”´ **3. Build from logic chips (No microcontroller at all)**
ğŸš« Not Arduino.

If you go this route:
- Youâ€™re building something Arduino-*like* using **discrete logic gates**, **flip-flops**, or even **TTL/CMOS chips**
- No real â€œArduinoâ€ functionality (no sketches, no `digitalWrite()`)

This would be more like building a **4-bit computer from scratch** â€” cool, but not Arduino-compatible.

---

## âš™ï¸ Quick Summary: DIY Arduino With Raw ATmega328P

| Part                         | Description                          |
|------------------------------|--------------------------------------|
| ATmega328P-PU                | Raw MCU                              |
| 16 MHz crystal               | Clock signal                         |
| 2 Ã— 22pF capacitors          | Crystal load caps                    |
| 10kÎ© resistor                | Pull-up on RESET                     |
| 0.1Î¼F capacitors             | Power rail decoupling (VCC/GND)      |
| FTDI/CH340/USB-TTL Adapter   | To upload code                       |
| 5V regulated power supply    | Stable operation                     |
